  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2229dcc5-a20b-4768-a831-e93d1f5e278f'
      });

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5925679686267073559">Before the nightmare, Yeong-hye and her husband lived an ordinary life. But when splintering, blood-soaked images start haunting her thoughts, Yeong-hye decides to purge her mind and renounce eating meat. In a country where societal mores are strictly obeyed, Yeong-hye's decision to embrace a more “plant-like” existence is a shocking act of subversion. And as her passive r</span>
  <span id="freeText5925679686267073559" style="display:none">Before the nightmare, Yeong-hye and her husband lived an ordinary life. But when splintering, blood-soaked images start haunting her thoughts, Yeong-hye decides to purge her mind and renounce eating meat. In a country where societal mores are strictly obeyed, Yeong-hye's decision to embrace a more “plant-like” existence is a shocking act of subversion. And as her passive rebellion manifests in ever more extreme and frightening forms, scandal, abuse, and estrangement begin to send Yeong-hye spiraling deep into the spaces of her fantasy. In a complete metamorphosis of both mind and body, her now dangerous endeavor will take Yeong-hye—impossibly, ecstatically, tragically—far from her once-known self altogether.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5925679686267073559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/15448757-lindsay"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1405877298i/15448757._UX100_CR0,19,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/15448757-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I think that In-hye was recalling contemplating suicide--the times she took a cord with her and went out on walks to the mountains, but on her final w<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I think that In-hye was recalling contemplating suicide--the times she took a cord with her and went out on walks to the mountains, but on her final walk, the same night her son dreamed of her as a white bird, she chose not to go through with it. Her section has a lot of descriptions of trees as cold and menacing. Her desire to kill herself and "abandon" her child was due to all the burdens she was carrying for others, and the toll it was taking on her psychologically. She also resents her sister and her husband for refusing these burdens and giving over to the strange desires plaguing them. In the final scene she is accompanying her sister, and tells her sister that she, too has dreams, but that they are just dreams and she can't give herself completely over to them. Then she stares defiantly at the trees.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/18285513-lexie"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1374254844i/18285513._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/18285513-lexie">Lexie</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I live in Korea and came here as a vegetarian. I work in the public school system and we eat the cafeteria food together. At first I would pack my own<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I live in Korea and came here as a vegetarian. I work in the public school system and we eat the cafeteria food together. At first I would pack my own food and I was always being bombared with questions. <br /><br />Yes, my Korean co-workers were really concerned about my health. They just thought it was curious of me to intentionally make the choice to avoid eating a whole food group. Everytime I got sick they would imply it was in part due to my poor diet choices! <br /><br />Also, food sharing is such a big part of Korean culture! In my experience I felt that if everyone was eating from the same dish, it was considered slightly rude to reject it. This made dinner outings quite difficult when I was the only vegetarian at a table! One co-teacher had told me that to reject a meal is to reject an offer of friendship. <br /><br />Of course there is the Buddhist population and they have some lovely vegetarian restaurants, however they usually serve "temple food" rather than modern vegetarian creations. They also serve vegetarian meals at the temples.<br /><br />This book takes it to another extreme of course, but I personally did find it kind of comical haha just because of how meat loving Koreans tend to be. :)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_1706158403" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1706158403" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1706158403?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=contemporary-lit">contemporary-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=veganism-and-vegetarianism">veganism-and-vegetarianism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=man-booker-prize-winners">man-booker-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1706158403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12739883839360506985">This book wasn’t a casual read for me. This is deeply personal to my own beliefs. I’m a vegan. This book sung a song that I heard in my marrow; it made me realise so much. <br/><br/>As a vegan I’ve experienced some of the things that I witnessed here. I can relate to it. I’ve lived it. I’ve been called a heretic. I’ve lost friends over it, and experienced much social awkwardness just for my lifestyle choice. I’m not pushy with my beliefs. Sometimes all it takes is a mention of the word “vegetarian” to be </span>
  <span id="freeText12739883839360506985" style="display:none">This book wasn’t a casual read for me. This is deeply personal to my own beliefs. I’m a vegan. This book sung a song that I heard in my marrow; it made me realise so much. <br /><br />As a vegan I’ve experienced some of the things that I witnessed here. I can relate to it. I’ve lived it. I’ve been called a heretic. I’ve lost friends over it, and experienced much social awkwardness just for my lifestyle choice. I’m not pushy with my beliefs. Sometimes all it takes is a mention of the word “vegetarian” to be received with utter hostility or blankness. This review isn’t about the ethics involved with the diet. That’s not important to this story. What’s important is how people can react to someone different to themselves. The vegetarian in here acts as a metaphor for individual life choices. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>“I was convinced that there was more going on here than a simple case of vegetarianism.”</i>
</b> <br /><br />For “the vegetarian,” being different to the world leads to social isolation and feelings of utter despair. Her husband is utterly useless in every regard. He is the sort of man who simply doesn’t deserve a wife. This no equal partnership, but the wife living to serve the man’s needs. It’s all about finding the right people, the accepting people in society. And this comes far too late and in far too a meek form to have any lasting effect on her: it came when she was already lost. The self-destructive behaviour isn’t testimony to a vegetarian diet. I speak from experience, these can be very healthy. It’s an act of rebellion against a society that refused to be warm to someone who didn’t conform. <br /><br /><i>“the sight of her lying there utterly without resistance, yet armored by the power of her own renunciation, was so intense as to bring tears to his eyes.” </i><br /><br />But, this is only the premise of the novel. It’s also about sexual desire, the unwavering power of lust and sheer emotional enthralment. However, it’s not about the body as an object of sexual desire; it’s not about the attractiveness, or the unattractiveness, of the female or male form; that’s just meat: it’s about the power of the individual: it’s about the power of an idealistic free spirit. And this is what drew me to the book. The lust in here is freedom. It is the ability to make one’s own life choice and live in harmony with the rest of the world. Labels don’t matter. Restrictions don’t matter. What matters is the freedom to be who you are and what you are. Whether or not this is a vegetarian, homosexual, transgender, a Buddhist Monk or a Christian isn’t overly important. What matters is choice. The vegetarian in here serves as a metaphor, a rallying cry, for a more accepting world. It’s a brutal reminder of the narrowmindedness that infects this planet towards those that fall through the cracks of society. <br /><br />Some readers may see the exploration of mental illness, though what I see is mental illness born of sheer social isolation and spiritual depravation. If a person belongs nowhere, and those that are supposed to be closest to her ignore her, then only maladaptive thinking can occur. Only detrimental cognitive functioning will be born. What “the Vegetarian” needed in here was someone to understand her individuality and to respect it. Instead the coldness she receives sends her own a downward spiral of delusional fantasy, very poetic fantasy. <br /><br />This is a book like no other.<br /><br />___________________________________<br /><br />You can connect with me on social media via <a href="https://linktr.ee/sean.ultra_vegan1" rel="nofollow noopener">My Linktree</a>.<br />__________________________________</span>
  <a data-text-id="12739883839360506985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1584960443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11234167160611381643">Update: Han Kang's book, "The Vegetarian", is the Man Booker international winner <br/><br/><br/>Wow...****<br/>****ZERO spoilers<br/> I'm going to share my experience....and hope to find other readers to discuss this book with later. <br/><br/>I couldn't 'not' read this in anything less than one sitting.<br/>If I keep thinking about this book, not only will I have a knot in my stomach as I do now...but I just might find myself crying my eyes out!<br/><br/>I haven't felt so many intense emotions from a book in a long time.<br/>Zillions of thoughts f</span>
  <span id="freeText11234167160611381643" style="display:none">Update: Han Kang's book, "The Vegetarian", is the Man Booker international winner <br /><br /><br />Wow...****<br />****ZERO spoilers<br /> I'm going to share my experience....and hope to find other readers to discuss this book with later. <br /><br />I couldn't 'not' read this in anything less than one sitting.<br />If I keep thinking about this book, not only will I have a knot in my stomach as I do now...but I just might find myself crying my eyes out!<br /><br />I haven't felt so many intense emotions from a book in a long time.<br />Zillions of thoughts flooded my mind from when our daughter was anorexic. At age 9, Katy came home from school one day and said, "I no longer eat meat".... which was just the beginning of her food eliminations. <br />This book goes beyond anything I've ever read on the topic of eating disorders...(there's more....exploring a range of themes)<br /><br />The writing is MASTERFUL...with many gorgeous passages. <br />I'm not sure this book is for everyone....but if you think you can handle minimal<br />disturbing abuse and horror images...there is enough 'meat' (pun), in this book to keep the cerebral brain doing somersaults for months....leaving behind many questions to think about. <br /><br />*......A tidbit which might be useful information for those who read this. <br />"A Mongolian mark is a bluish birthmark, very common among infants of color though uncommon among Caucasian infants, that typically disappears by the time a child is around five". Having this information would have 'helped' me feel less confused when I first started reading part II .<br />The Vegetarian", is a novel of three-linked novellas. POWERFUL!!<br /><br />Thank You Crown Publishing, Netgalley, and Han Kang....( I think *Kang* is kinda a genius)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11234167160611381643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes">Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=1-star">1-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=year-2017">year-2017</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1826254043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7187144432271585421"><b>Nope.</b> <br/><br/>This is the story of two sisters & their husbands & one woman's tendency toward violent, graphic dreams. <br/><br/>Honestly, I'm not sure what I was supposed to take from this book & I'm not going to pretend for the sake of sounding intelligent or profound that I "got it."<br/><br/>I absolutely <u>did not enjoy</u> reading this, and I'm thankful it was over quickly. The men in this book are repulsive & selfish, and I could not identify with either sister. The lack of straightforward communication between all of the </span>
  <span id="freeText7187144432271585421" style="display:none"><b>Nope.</b> <br /><br />This is the story of two sisters &amp; their husbands &amp; one woman's tendency toward violent, graphic dreams. <br /><br />Honestly, I'm not sure what I was supposed to take from this book &amp; I'm not going to pretend for the sake of sounding intelligent or profound that I "got it."<br /><br />I absolutely <u>did not enjoy</u> reading this, and I'm thankful it was over quickly. The men in this book are repulsive &amp; selfish, and I could not identify with either sister. The lack of straightforward communication between all of the characters was <b>consistently annoying.</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>And maybe that was the point, I don't know.</i>
</b><br /><br />Maybe I was supposed to feel irritated throughout the whole novel?<br /><br />Maybe the outright objectification of both women by every single male they come into contact with was supposed to speak to societal expectations between men &amp; women?<br /><br />Maybe the lack of agency both women seem to have was supposed to encompass widespread lack of agency among women in general? <br /><br />Maybe all the weird, sexually graphic imagery was a parallel for the mass produced &amp; detached quality of the porn industry? <br /><br />Maybe my lack of experience with the culture prevents me from understanding the higher meaning here?<br /><br /><b>Truly, I don't know.</b><br /><br />Regardless of the intended message, or what others seem to have taken from this novel, reading this was frustrating. I don't think the method of delivery worked for me. You win some, you lose some, I guess.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1663765163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1663765163" />
    <a title="Felice Laverne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50404233-felice-laverne"><img alt="Felice Laverne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548611171p2/50404233.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1663765163?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felice Laverne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felice Laverne" href="/user/show/50404233-felice-laverne">Felice Laverne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=reviewed-on-barnesandnoble">reviewed-on-barnesandnoble</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=full-review">full-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=cultural-surveys">cultural-surveys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=multicultural-fiction">multicultural-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=mental-illness">mental-illness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=reviewed-on-amazon">reviewed-on-amazon</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1663765163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8973190384366103737"><i>“Though the ostensible reason for her not wanting Yeong-hye to be discharged, the reason that she gave the doctor, was this worry about a possible relapse, now she was able to admit to herself what had really been going on. She was no longer able to cope with all that her sister reminded her of. She’d been unable to forgive her for soaring alone over a boundary she herself could never bring herself to cross, unable to forgive that magnificent irresponsibility that had enabled Yeong-hye to shuck </i></span>
  <span id="freeText8973190384366103737" style="display:none"><i>“Though the ostensible reason for her not wanting Yeong-hye to be discharged, the reason that she gave the doctor, was this worry about a possible relapse, now she was able to admit to herself what had really been going on. She was no longer able to cope with all that her sister reminded her of. She’d been unable to forgive her for soaring alone over a boundary she herself could never bring herself to cross, unable to forgive that magnificent irresponsibility that had enabled Yeong-hye to shuck off social constraints and leave her behind, still a prisoner. And before Yeong-hye had broken those bars, she’d never even known they were there…”</i><br /><br />	Wow, what can I say about this one except “wow?”<br />	<br /> <i>The Vegetarian</i> by Han Kang was everything that we love about Korean and Japanese literature and art—and that’s exactly what this work was: art. Here you will find what we have come to know, to love and to expect from authors in this genre who write in this vein: the vibrancy, the subtle magical realism, the commanding usage of words and the elusive, sinister nature that is unique to these works—all embedded within an established culture of history and mores that has survived and developed for millennia longer than most others. <br /><br /> <i>The Vegetarian</i> read with a delicious ominousness that was as subtle as a shadow, like a breath at your neck. It was that subtly that made the read so taunt and disquieting, and there was a strange, magical realism to it that almost read like Haruki Murakami’s <i>1Q84</i> (no shock there, as they both seem to have been influenced by Kafka). As a work of short literary form (it’s under 200 pages), it was unusual, among other reasons, in that it was told from three different perspectives with almost no perspective from the novel’s subject, Yeong-hye. We see how her vegetarianism, which later leads into a kind of manic catatonia, affects first her callous and at times sexually abusive husband, then her brother-in-law who becomes completely enthralled with her sexually because of her Mongolian mark, and her sister who is the last one standing when Yeong-hye’s psyche begins to peel away.	<br /><br /> In addition to the serious topics that <i>The Vegetarian</i> brushed up against: the effect of cultural mores on women, body image, conformism, familial ties and abuse, and, of course, mental illness that ultimately culminated in a way that I could never reveal without spoiling it for you—this was also a tale of family dysfunction. It was a tale of familial ties that were severed painfully, of violent confrontations and realizations, of physical and emotional starvation, and a parable about the woman, the vegetarian, at the center of it all.<br /><br /> <i>The Vegetarian</i> was sensual, and it meandered toward its climax in a way that was both unsettling and prophetic. It was allegory elevated to the highest level of art, raised to the level of surrealism. Honestly, this may or may not work for everyone. You have to suspend your past experiences with reading (outside of this genre) in order to enjoy this one. That much is, honestly, a must. The change in tenses and POVs worked well. And even this technique, this simple process of sentence writing that we learn in grade school, was elevated: the tenses of sentences shifted noticeably, particularly the closer that it came to dénouement, a jolting but brilliant allusion to this descent into mental illness and personal violence, which added to the mystical element of this novel.<br /><br />	Han Kang produced a work, her first to be seen here in the U.S., that was so unhinged, so mystifying, that at times it would slither from your grasp. I had to sit and reflect on several of the passages for a few minutes—not because they were ill-written, but because they were both profound and often just outside of my immediate mental grasp, and that was a wonderful thing. It was an effect that I look for in modern-day writing—that disquietingly ungraspable moment.<br /><br />	<i>Yeong-hye’s voice, which came to her while she was suspended in that halfway state between sleep and wakefulness, was low and warm at first, then innocent like that of a young child, but the last part was mangled, a distorted animal sound. Her eyes snapped open in fright, and she was stung by a waking hatred the likes of which she’d never felt before, before being thrown back into sleep. This time she was standing in from of the bathroom mirror. In the reflection, blood was trickling from her left eye. She quickly reached up to wipe the blood away, but somehow her reflection in the mirror didn’t move an inch, only stood there, blood running from a staring eye.</i><br /><br />	<i>The Vegetarian</i> was unconventional. It broke away from the molds that we find ourselves encumbered in with typical fiction. Here you will not find the typical “rising action, climax, falling action” formula that we’ve become so accustomed to, that we’ve grown to expect and to lean into, though we know how it’ll all end in the end. There may or may not have been some issues with translation, but if there were, it wasn't overtly noticeable to me. I found the translation obstacles, where applicable, to be mild at worst. I would recommend this read to anyone who's ready to move away from the conventional, and to anyone already familiar or ready to become familiar with this genre of writing. Honestly, this read left me a little speechless, so you’ll have to excuse the less-than-customary word count here. Definitely, take that as a compliment in the highest sense. 5 stars. ***** <br /><br /><b>FOLLOW ME HERE:</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49185491-the-other-americans?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=tl37nwckP6&amp;rank=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodreads</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/queenbee_fifi" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/thefelicelaverne/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.amazon.com/Other-Americans-Novel-Felice-Laverne/dp/1672094488/ref=sr_1_1?crid=25JA8C7GHLFY8&amp;keywords=the+other+americans+felice+laverne&amp;qid=1579649731&amp;sprefix=the+other+americans+felice%2Caps%2C208&amp;sr=8-1" rel="nofollow noopener">Get a Copy of My Book</a> | <a href="https://www.artanddecoagency.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Editing, Author Coaching, Submit Your Book to Me</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="8973190384366103737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1521374552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15363501456757300803"><b>Update #2: I've gotten enough comments from dudes for me to update this review. Oddly enough, it's just dudes, too. No woman has commented about how I'm wrong about Kang saying the book is an allegory for South Korea. And I can only guess it's because women ACTUALLY READ THE FUCKIN DESCRIPTION. From the dust jacket AND the product description here on Goodreads:<br/><br/><i>"A disturbing, yet beautifully composed narrative told in three parts, The Vegetarian is an allegorical novel about modern day South Kore</i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText15363501456757300803" style="display:none"><b>Update #2: I've gotten enough comments from dudes for me to update this review. Oddly enough, it's just dudes, too. No woman has commented about how I'm wrong about Kang saying the book is an allegory for South Korea. And I can only guess it's because women ACTUALLY READ THE FUCKIN DESCRIPTION. From the dust jacket AND the product description here on Goodreads:<br /><br /><i>"A disturbing, yet beautifully composed narrative told in three parts, The Vegetarian is an allegorical novel about modern day South Korea..."</i><br /><br />Come on, guys. You can't be this fucking dense.</b><br /><br /><b>UPDATE: Since reading this book, I've seen numerous reviews from women who claim this book speaks to the hidden violence and hate women keep in their heart. I am, of course, not a woman and do not claim to know the struggles of the opposite sex, but I am willing to admit that I might have missed something a woman might easily find. As stated in my review, the author herself has said the book is an allegory for present-day South Korea. When discussing the book, Kang mentions nothing about any female metaphors, but if women are finding them, that's cool. That means Han Kang's art is affecting a large group of people in ways the author didn't intend. I find that fascinating and exceedingly cool. I, however, did not enjoy the book as a whole and stand by my review and rating. I've added this update to perhaps persuade you ladies who enjoy literary fiction to give this novel a try. I'm a dude and will only ever be a dude, and dudes are notoriously short-sighted when it comes to the ladies. So give it a go and see if it speaks to you as it has so many others. Now on to the review... </b><br /><br /><i>The Vegetarian</i> is my first real disappointment of 2016. Too bad, too, because it was going so well.<br /><br />Here we have a story that starts off creepy and interesting. Then, about 60 pages in, it slams face-first into a tree and, like Sonny Bono, never recovers. (Too soon?) The first third of the book (the novel is broken up into three 60-page sections) is told in first-person past tense. The second part switches to third-person past tense, and while still an interesting part of the story, caused the narrative to come to a grinding halt while we have to get to know a whole other person only to relive entire sections of the first sixty pages. And then, finally, in the third part, the author swings into third-person present tense and completely shits the bed. We're forced to get to know someone else that doesn't matter and relive certain aspects of both the first and second parts. <br /><br />This is a novella stretched into a 188-page novel. Supposedly it is an allegorical study of present-day Korea, but I cannot comment on the accuracy of that statement. All I know is, I was onboard until the author started switching POVs and tenses and repeating shit ad nauseum, as if this were the novelization of <i>Vantage Point</i>. Anyone remember that movie? The non-crazy Quaid brother plays a secret service officer, or some shit? The entire story is only about 15 minutes long, but it's told from the perspectives of like eight motherfuckers? Oh well. It's an all right movie. Better than this book, at any rate.<br /><br />Holy kitten nipples, where the fuck was I?<br /><br />Oh yeah. Shit book. Well, that's not fair. It's not a completely shit book. The first 60 pages are rad. The next sixty pages are okay. The last 60 are pointless and boring. Overall, a very unbalanced read. Had I paid for this one instead of getting a review copy from Crown Publishing in exchange for the review you are currently reading, I probably would've been pissed.<br /><br />In summation: Allegorical or not, this book failed on certain levels for me. The last time I saw something go this bad this quickly was when we found out Jared from Subway was forcing underage boys to eat his footlong. Not recommended.<br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: I wanna like you, but you're you, and I don't like you.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15363501456757300803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1820389178" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1820389178" />
    <a title="Justin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2795921-justin"><img alt="Justin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528849864p2/2795921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1820389178?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin" href="/user/show/2795921-justin">Justin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1820389178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7056578486070632491">Honestly, in this case, I'm much more interested in reading other people's reviews of the book rather than writing my own. I just feel like there are so many layers here, so many things that need to be discussed, so many unique interpretations, so many questions. <br/><br/>The first third of The Vegetarian is very Kafka-esque. It has a very Metamorphosis vibe to it. Maybe a little bit of Bird Box to give you a more contemporary example. It was dark. It was weird. It was bleak. It felt like every sentence </span>
  <span id="freeText7056578486070632491" style="display:none">Honestly, in this case, I'm much more interested in reading other people's reviews of the book rather than writing my own. I just feel like there are so many layers here, so many things that need to be discussed, so many unique interpretations, so many questions. <br /><br />The first third of The Vegetarian is very Kafka-esque. It has a very Metamorphosis vibe to it. Maybe a little bit of Bird Box to give you a more contemporary example. It was dark. It was weird. It was bleak. It felt like every sentence belonged there. There wasn't any filler or fluff to bulk up the length. I was all in, too. I was waiting to solve the mystery and figure out what the heck was going on. <br /><br />Then, the second act hits and I realized this book is something completely different than what I expected. I was mildly annoyed at the kind of abrasive shift away from what I wanted the book to be, but once I got over myself and continued to read, I loved the book for completely different reasons. I didn't even know why I liked it really. I kinda felt awkward at times, but then I got over myself again. <br /><br />The third and final act doesn't answer a lot of questions. It actually leaves you with a lot of questions, but not about the story- more about deeper issues like what it is to be human, innocence, and violence. Nothing about not eating animals. Don't let the title fool you. The Vegetarian takes things to a much deeper level than if killing animals is wrong. I barely remember that being discussed. <br /><br />Here's the best way I can break this down for you. Ever watched a good foreign film? You know how in a lot of foreign films there aren't millions of dollars invested into CGI and fhe actors and actresses look like real people? The movie is focused on the dialogue and the story rather than looking and sounding amazing. At the end, you feel something inside and you're thinking, man, I didn't know movies could be like this. I haven't seen anything like this before. That's how The Vegetarian is- translated into English from a South Korean author that doesn't look or feel like any book you've read before. You put it down and you're like, man, I didn't know books could be like this. I haven't read anything like this before. <br /><br />Eat animals or don't. Whatever. But check this one out. It's under 200 pages. Spend a Sunday afternoon with it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7056578486070632491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1554096865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1554096865" />
    <a title="Megan Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31853583-megan-johnson"><img alt="Megan Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523045365p2/31853583.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1554096865?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Johnson" href="/user/show/31853583-megan-johnson">Megan Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">international book lovers, and anyone looking for something different/memorable</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31853583-megan-johnson?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1554096865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10526597415417955185">Han Kang's novel, 'The Vegetarian,' tells the story of Yeong-hye. Having recently had a dream that has convinced her to cease eating any meat whatsoever, and finds that such a decision is affect nearly all aspects of her life. Her family is trying to force her to eat meat, the relationships that once surrounded her are falling apart, and everyone is questioning whether she is insane. The thing is, she just might be losing her mind - and all because of a dream.<br/><br/>This book isn't super long. In fact,</span>
  <span id="freeText10526597415417955185" style="display:none">Han Kang's novel, 'The Vegetarian,' tells the story of Yeong-hye. Having recently had a dream that has convinced her to cease eating any meat whatsoever, and finds that such a decision is affect nearly all aspects of her life. Her family is trying to force her to eat meat, the relationships that once surrounded her are falling apart, and everyone is questioning whether she is insane. The thing is, she just might be losing her mind - and all because of a dream.<br /><br />This book isn't super long. In fact, it reads like a novella, so it's easy to consume and fast paced enough that about the time you feel really into it, it's over. It's not a bad thing though because there is a LOT packed into this little story. Despite being a story that is explicitly about Yeong-hye, it is actually never told directly from her perspective. Instead, we are give about 60 pages a piece from her husband, her brother-in-law, and her sister. The oddest part about this formatting is, unlike many book of this type, the perspectives do not overlap. Some take place at the time of Yeong-hye's decision to become a vegetarian, and others take place years after the fact.<br /><br />To be totally honest, this book is weird. It might actually be the weirdest book I've ever read. But there's still something beautiful about it. It's an honest look at mental illness and how it affects not only that individual, but also everyone that cares about them. For those who have dealt with abuse, it's not as cut and dry to understand why mental illness affects them. Instead, it's about learning how to cope and manage, but also when to let go of those who hold you back.<br /><br />As I mentioned before, this book is a roller coaster ride, especially in the beginning. I sat down to read this expecting to read for maybe 20 minutes later but looked at the clock an hour later and realized that I was so involved in the story that I didn't want to stop reading. It's sad, depressing, at times fun, but most of all it's probably the most thought-provoking novel I have ever read.<br /><br /><b>My rating:</b> I give it 3 stars not because I didn't like it or I don't think you should read it, but because I found the pacing of the book to be distracting. It starts off energetic, fast-paced, and almost manic. During the middle, it takes a turn and reads more as a desperate plea for approval or attention. And in the end, it's detailed, slower, and tired. I don't know whether this was an intentional decision, but for me as a reader it meant that every 60 pages or so I was forced to feel like I was adjusting to a new writing style.<br /><br /><b>Who should read it?:</b> If you enjoy international novels, this is an excellent choice. I have been told that those who have been deeply affected by the decision to become a vegetarian have loved this story. Or anyone who wants to read something that is so unlike anything else out there, that there is no way they will forget this book.<br /><br /><p>
  I was provided with a free copy of this book in order to conduct this review.
</p><br /><br /><br /><br /><a href="http://www.tipsywriter.com" rel="nofollow noopener">WEBSITE</a> | <a href="http://www.twitter.com/tipsy_writer" rel="nofollow noopener">TWITTER</a> | <a href="http://www.facebook.com/tipsywriter" rel="nofollow noopener">FACEBOOK</a> | <a href="http://www.instagram.com/tipsywriter" rel="nofollow noopener">INSTAGRAM</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10526597415417955185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

This is a book about characters whose backstories and full character arcs aren't really explored, so it was jarring to read a character-heavy book whose main focus is on a woman that we never even see the perspective of. <div id="review_3392989198" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3392989198" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3392989198?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846276033" href="/book/show/27191166-the-vegetarian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=food">food</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=feminism-patriarchy-gender-roles">feminism-patriarchy-gender-roles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=mental-health-victorian-madness">mental-health-victorian-madness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=relationships-twisted">relationships-twisted</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=sexuality-gender-lgbtq">sexuality-gender-lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=china-japan-asia">china-japan-asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3392989198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13780587032205819292"><b>How to review this cold, voyeuristic, sexy, ugly, beautiful, disturbing novella?</b><br/>The single story is told in three sections, from three points of view - in three different genres: from a domestic drama of a marriage suddenly under strain, through erotica, to madness and borderline magical-realism. But we never hear from the subject herself. <br/><br/>At the end… I felt adrift, rather than rooted. Intrigued, moved, and pleasantly bemused. <br/><br/><b>Triggers</b>: This book is probably unusable for those in the depths of e</span>
  <span id="freeText13780587032205819292" style="display:none"><b>How to review this cold, voyeuristic, sexy, ugly, beautiful, disturbing novella?</b><br>The single story is told in three sections, from three points of view - in three different genres: from a domestic drama of a marriage suddenly under strain, through erotica, to madness and borderline magical-realism. But we never hear from the subject herself. <br><br>At the end… I felt adrift, rather than rooted. Intrigued, moved, and pleasantly bemused. <br><br><b>Triggers</b>: This book is probably unusable for those in the depths of eating disorders, body dysmorphia, or suicidal ideation. It’s not really about vegetarianism. <br><br><b>1. The Vegetarian</b><br><br>It starts simply: Yeong-hye is a young South Korean woman. She has been married for a few years to Mr Cheong, who narrates the first part. He is very aware of her ordinariness; it’s part of what made her suitable. <br><br>“<i>If there wasn’t any special attraction, nor did any particular drawbacks present themselves, and therefore there was no reason for the two of us not to get married.</i>”<br><br>When Yeong-hye has her first vivid and disturbing dream about butchered animals, she becomes a vegetarian. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593552061i/29741583._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Abattoir</i> (<a href="https://www.thebureauinvestigates.com/stories/2017-09-19/blowing-the-whistle-on-the-meat-industry" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br>“<i>I ate too much meat. The lives of the animals I ate have all lodged there.</i>” [on her chest]<br><br>This change is shocking and disgusting to Mr Cheong, shameful in front of his colleagues, and anathema to her traditional family. Conformity is required, individualism reviled. <br><br>The nearest Mr Cheong comes to demonstrating any passion is when he describes, in almost fetishistic terms, how delicious his wife's meat-based cooking used to be. Yeong-hye may be becoming anorexic or delusional, but he shows neither sympathy nor curiosity - just anger and resentment at the effect on him. <br><br><b>2. Mongolian Mark</b><br><br>(A <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongolian_spot" rel="nofollow noopener">Mongolian mark</a> is a type of grey birth mark at the base of the spine, that usually disappears by age 5 and almost always before puberty.) <br><br>The middle section opens at a dance show with an ox-blood coloured curtain.<br>“<i>The carnality, the pure sensuality of this image, was nothing short of monstrous.</i>”<br><br>It focuses on the unnamed husband of Yeong-hye’s sister. He’s an unsuccessful video artist, emasculated by his wife’s success: “<i>their only shared business was their child</i>”.<br><br>His dramatic inspiration for a new artwork is personal, passionate, and transgressive. The story transmogrifies into artistic erotica, with undertones of paedophilic desires.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593552061i/29741584._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>“Magnolia”, by Cecilia Paredes</i> (<a href="https://www.businessinsider.com/cecilia-paredes-wallpaper-art--2012-2?r=US&amp;IR=T#dreaming-rose-2009-3" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br>“<i>The monochrome world, entirely devoid of the colours he was now experiencing, had had a calmness that was beautiful in its way… All of his energy was taken up in trying to cope with the excitement, the heightened awareness of living in the present moment.</i>”<br><br><b>3. Flaming Trees</b><br><br>“<i>She had merely absorbed all her suffering inside her.</i>”<br><br>The final section, narrated by In-hye (Yeong-hye’s older sister), mostly in the present tense, is a darker exploration of change, humanity, family ties, duty, and madness.<br><br>“<i>Soon now, words and thoughts will disappear.</i>”<br><br>Is she like Kafka's <b>The Hunger Artist</b>? (See my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324204" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593552061i/29741585._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Woman doing handstand in a forest.</i> (<a href="https://www.alamy.com/young-woman-practicing-yoga-in-forest-doing-handstand-against-tree-trunk-image183135792.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Men versus sisters</b><br><br>“<i>Hand, foot, tongue, gaze, all weapons from which nothing is safe.</i>”<br><br>This felt like a book written by a man until part way into the middle section. I think that’s because it was told by/from male points of view, one of them judgemental, controlling, and cold, and both objectifying women. <br><br>“<i>It was a body that made one want to rest one’s gaze quietly upon it.</i>”<br><br>The final section by In-hye was different. Although the story is ostensibly about Yeong-hye, the relationship between the sisters is at the heart of it all.<br><br>•	One is serene, self-contained, and possibly brave: <br>“<i>She radiated energy, like a tree that grows in the wilderness, denuded and solitary.</i>”<br>•	The other is successful, dutiful, conventional: <br>“<i>The kind of woman whose goodness is oppressive</i>”.<br>Both have deep inner strength, and they are tied by blood and by the shared suffix of their names (whereas the men are Mr Cheong and... who knows what In-hye's husband is called?). <br><br>•	How much does one owe family - and what happens when there are conflicting needs?<br>•	What’s the toll of guilt, betrayal, and jealousy?<br>•	To what extent can one break free of genes and expectations, and at what cost?<br><br><b>Imagery</b><br><br>The dreams, described in short italic passages, involve blood, flesh, and eyes. <br>Blood and eyes recur throughout.<br>As do breasts, birds, and trees.<br>I felt there was more to these symbols than the obvious, but I'm not sure what. <br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>•	“The kiss was a palimpsest of memories.”<br><br>•	“Dreams overlaid with dreams, a palimpsest of horror.”<br><br>•	“With my round breasts, I’m okay… So why do they keep on shrinking?... Why are my edges all sharpening - what am I going to gouge?”<br><br>•	“It was impossible to tell whether or not he was pleased to see his father.” Of a five-year old. Tragic.<br><br>•	“As if there were a wire linking her tongue with his body, every time that little pink tongue darted out [licking shaved ice] he found himself flinching as though from an electric shock.”<br><br>•	“This was the body of a beautiful young woman… yet it was a body from which all the desire had been eliminated…. What she had renounced was the very life that her body represented. The sunlight that came splintering through the wide window… and the beauty of that body… was also ceaselessly splintering.”<br><br>•	“Her calm acceptance… made her seem to him something sacred. Whether human, animal or plant, she could not be called a ‘person’.”<br><br>•	“The brush was cold, and the sensation was ticklish yet numbing, a persistent, effectual caress.”<br><br>•	“She’d been unable to forgive her for soaring alone over a boundary she herself could never bring herself to cross,” <br><br>•	“She had never lived… she had done nothing but endure… Her life was no more than a ghostly pageant of exhausted endurance, no more real than a television drama.”<br><br>•	“Her voice had no weight… neither gloomy nor absent-minded… the quiet tone of someone who didn’t belong anywhere, someone who had passed into a border area between states of being.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593552191i/29741592._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>“Her bare feet kissing the tiles.”</i> (<a href="https://tiledevil.co.uk/blogs/style/are-floor-tiles-cold" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Notes about the original Korean novella</b><br><br>The three parts were originally published separately. I think a slightly firmer delineation of the parts is probably helpful. The disconcerting and dramatic change of viewpoint and genre is part of the appeal, and what makes this so unusual. <br><br>There's also controversy about the English translation, as highlighted in Nocturnalux 's comment below, and discussed in knowledgeable detail, with links to other articles, in the comments of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1182938971" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul's review</a>. <br><br>Is the English version I read barely recognisable version of the original, or are the criticisms themselves rooted in racist stereotypes?<br><br>Another good article, courtesy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1526609569" rel="nofollow noopener">Chinook's review</a> is <a href="https://koreaexpose.com/deborah-smith-translation-han-kang-novel-vegetarian/" rel="nofollow noopener">this one</a>. It picks out specific examples of clearcut "mistakes". But it concludes:<br>"And ultimately, Smith carried out perhaps the most important task of all: She successfully introduced a work of literature to people who might otherwise never have had a chance to read it. In that regard, Smith was faithful to the end."<br>I'm glad I was able to read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13780587032205819292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1640424514" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1640424514" />
    <a title="Adina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22189348-adina"><img alt="Adina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531917330p2/22189348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1640424514?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adina" href="/user/show/22189348-adina">Adina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=booker">booker</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=w-mwl-alternative">w-mwl-alternative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=korea">korea</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640424514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15707445716865000070"><i>“She was no longer able to cope with all that her sister reminded her of. She'd been unable to forgive her for soaring alone over a boundary she herself could never bring herself to cross, unable to forgive that magnificent irresponsibility that had enabled Yeong-hye to shuck off social constraints and leave her behind, still a prisoner. And before Yeong-hye had broken those bars, she'd never even known they were there.” </i><br/><br/>For a small book it took me a lot of time to finish. The reason is that I w</span>
  <span id="freeText15707445716865000070" style="display:none"><i>“She was no longer able to cope with all that her sister reminded her of. She'd been unable to forgive her for soaring alone over a boundary she herself could never bring herself to cross, unable to forgive that magnificent irresponsibility that had enabled Yeong-hye to shuck off social constraints and leave her behind, still a prisoner. And before Yeong-hye had broken those bars, she'd never even known they were there.” </i><br /><br />For a small book it took me a lot of time to finish. The reason is that I wanted to absorb every word while, in the same time, I had to stop periodically because the emotional loading that my heart accumulated became too much.<br /><br />Korean people are close to my heart and with every year that I go there and meet my business partners I believe I understand them more. The experience I have there made me feel deeply about this novel. It is tough and not always pleasant to be Korean, the traditions and the society puts pressure on each individual, especially on women, to comply and be responsible.<br /><br />During the conversation I had with various Koreans, I understood there still is the expectation that the woman should follow the men’s orders and to put the well-being of the family above hers, a theme that was present in this novel<br /><br />Another cultural shock that I had was about the education in Korea. A partner told me that he could not go on holiday with his family for the past 2-3 years because his child was in high-school and had to study for university, for an exam which will be only in another year. It seems that the competition there is so high and if the young Koreans want to get to a good university (a must if striving for a decent life) they need to study non-stop, without any fun in their life. Another partner was very preoccupied to register his child into a good kindergarten because if they missed that chance the child’s life could be a failure. They even have exams to be admitted to kindergarten. Can you imagine the pressure the children have to face from such an early age?… no wonder they feel trapped when they get older. I read an article in Economist about this problem which seems to be spread in many Asian countries. <br /><br />I read reviews that the book is not realistic… it is wrong. It is very realistic and I can see it happen, including the craze with the vegetarianism. <br /><br /><i>“The feeling that she had never really lived in this world caught her by surprise. It was a fact. She had never lived. Even as a child, as far back as she could remember, she had done nothing but endure. She had believed in her own inherent goodness, her humanity, and lived accordingly, never causing anyone harm. Her devotion to doing things the right way had been unflagging, all her successes had depended on it, and she would have gone on like that indefinitely. She didn't understand why, but faced with those decaying buildings and straggling grasses, she was nothing but a child who had never lived.”</i><br /><br /><br />***<br />What a better moment to read this than in Seoul while eating lots of Korean meat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15707445716865000070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3090691522" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2768147490769803857">HUH. *nods head slowly* I started this a few years ago, put it down with every intention to return, and finally did (in audiobook form). I left this feeling that I hadn't connected to the characters or story very much, but finding the world that was built and the ideas presented really interesting. I left it not wanting necessarily to recommend it to anyone but certainly wanting to write an essay about it to unpack what happened. HUH.</span>

<div id="review_2279805963" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2279805963" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer7890446356143079854">She's turned vegetarian... now what a ridiculous b!+ch!<br/><br/>Riveting adult fiction. What do I mean by "adult"? In middle school you would not have understood it; in high school you would have hated it. But adults acting as children, having crises in front of their community at high noon? Yeah. I've been indoctrinated. <br/><br/>Han Kang has a taut, very animalistic tale of madness and marriage to share with us. So... Let's eat!</span>

<div id="review_3374128679" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3374128679" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3374128679?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3374128679" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12459272658939874079">The author makes a point of starting the story by telling us it’s about an ordinary couple. The man, recognizing that he is nothing special, marries a woman whom he calls “the most run-of-the-mill woman in the world.”<br/><br/>The young woman, living in Seoul, starts having nightmares full of blood and hanging meat. She decides to become a vegetarian. Her husband is irate and even recruits her family to help him get her to start eating and cooking meat again. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591285325i/29593891._SX540_.jpg' width='440' height='390' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>That pressure and the continuing nightmares pu</span>
  <span id="freeText12459272658939874079" style="display:none">The author makes a point of starting the story by telling us it’s about an ordinary couple. The man, recognizing that he is nothing special, marries a woman whom he calls “the most run-of-the-mill woman in the world.”<br><br>The young woman, living in Seoul, starts having nightmares full of blood and hanging meat. She decides to become a vegetarian. Her husband is irate and even recruits her family to help him get her to start eating and cooking meat again. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591285325i/29593891._SX540_.jpg" width="440" height="390" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>That pressure and the continuing nightmares push her further toward an almost plant-like existence. She takes her clothes off to get sun and runs into the forest and stands in the rain.<br>She hardly sleeps; she become anorexic and skeletal. Her brother-in-law, a video artist, sexually exploits her. <br><br>We learn about her abusive father, and maybe some of her issues had roots in that abuse. <br><br>There is good writing, such as this passage about her brother-in-law:<br><br>“…even after they were married he still looked perpetually worn out. He was always busy with his own things, and during what little time he did spend at home he looked more like a traveler putting up there for a night than a man in his own home. His silence had the heavy mass of rock and the tenacious resistance of rubber, particularly when his art wasn’t going well.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591285325i/29593892._SX540_.jpg" width="440" height="330" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The author (b. 1970) has written a half-dozen novels of which three have been translated into English. This book, The Vegetarian, won the 2016 Man Booker prize for translations. It was also picked as one of the 10 Best Books of 2016 by the NYTimes Book Review. I also happen to be reading her The White book, which was shortlisted for the 2018 Man Booker, so I’ll post that review shortly. <br><br>Photo of Seoul from beltandroad.news<br>The author from nytimes.com </span>

<div id="review_3068388007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3068388007" />
    <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea"><img alt="Amalia Gkavea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559158764p2/56789747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3068388007?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalia Gkavea" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea">Amalia Gkavea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=south-korea">south-korea</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=women-in-translation">women-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=psychology-mentality">psychology-mentality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3068388007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12566362319090595389"><i>
  <b>''Dark woods. No people. The sharp-pointed leaves in the trees, my torn feet. This place, almost remembered, but I'm lost now. Frightened. Cold. Across the frozen ravine, a red barn-like building. Straw matting flapping limp across the door. Roll it up and I'm inside, it's inside. A long bamboo stick strung with great blood-red gashes of meat, there's no end to the meat, and no exit. Blood in my mouth, blood-soaked clothes washed onto my skin.''</b>
</i><br/><br/>A young woman decides to stop eating meat to th</span>
  <span id="freeText12566362319090595389" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>''Dark woods. No people. The sharp-pointed leaves in the trees, my torn feet. This place, almost remembered, but I'm lost now. Frightened. Cold. Across the frozen ravine, a red barn-like building. Straw matting flapping limp across the door. Roll it up and I'm inside, it's inside. A long bamboo stick strung with great blood-red gashes of meat, there's no end to the meat, and no exit. Blood in my mouth, blood-soaked clothes washed onto my skin.''</b>
</i><br /><br />A young woman decides to stop eating meat to the dismay of her husband and her parents who refuse to agree to an act that is ''out'' of the established system. The only people who stand by her are her caring sister and her brother-in-law whose motives are extremely controversial. The consequences of her decision will prove a living nightmare in a social circle that discards the different, the ''unnatural''.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“Why, is it such a bad thing to die?”</b>
</i><br /><br />Han Kang presents a dark, contemporary fable through three POVs corresponding to the three parts of the novel. The first part is narrated by the husband, the second one by her brother-in-law and the final part (the section that makes the story so powerful, in my opinion) is viewed through the reflections of her sister. The young woman is denied a perspective to accentuate her isolation within her social circle that is unable to understand that things have changed. The voices of the three characters are distinct. The husband is a heartless man, her lover is confused and the sister is a compassionate, understanding woman who becomes the sole support to a soul that wants to live by her rules.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“The feeling that she had never really lived in this world caught her by surprise. It was a fact. She had never lived. Even as a child, as far back as she could remember, she had done nothing but endure.”</b>
</i><br /><br />Kang's writing is beautiful, raw and poetic. Even though the heroine doesn't share her thoughts with us, we come to understand her clearly and I deeply sympathised with her. She wants to love according to her choices, to feel loves, to defy a tyrannical father and a pathetic husband. The story is a violent, realistic allegory that has nothing to do with vegetarianism. I couldn't care less about it. For me, what matters is what comes out of our mouths and not what goes in and pseudo-verbal revolutions do not amaze me. I don't buy, sorry. <br /><br />Kang weaves a tale that is multilayered and powerful. Sensuality, identity, independence, Art, Psychology, Nature, instinct. These are the ingredients of one of the finest books of our times. Han Kang created a contemporary masterpiece. Yes, it's dark and ''strange'' and often hard to stomach. These are the characteristics of the best books. And this is a formidable story of obsession, independence and womanhood and a worthy winner of the Man Booker International Prize.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>''Dreams of murder...<br />Murderer of murdered...hazy distinctions, boundaries wearing thin. Familiarity bleeds into strangeness, certainty becomes impossible. Only the violence is vivid enough to stick. A sound, the elasticity of the instant when the metal struck the victim's head...the shadow that crumpled and fell gleams cold in the darkness.''</b>
</i> <br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com/">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12566362319090595389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2669889396" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2669889396" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2669889396?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2669889396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10105011908787093495">A disturbing novel about anorexia, patriarchy, and abuse, <i>The Vegetarian</i> explores the emotional toll of violence against women. The novel follows Yeong-hye, a young South Korean woman trapped in a loveless marriage, as she stops eating meat and starts to starve herself and rebel against her callous family after having a blood-soaked nightmare. Yeong-hye’s behavior grows increasingly erratic and self-destructive across the novel’s three equal-length parts, in turn narrated from the perspectives o</span>
  <span id="freeText10105011908787093495" style="display:none">A disturbing novel about anorexia, patriarchy, and abuse, <i>The Vegetarian</i> explores the emotional toll of violence against women. The novel follows Yeong-hye, a young South Korean woman trapped in a loveless marriage, as she stops eating meat and starts to starve herself and rebel against her callous family after having a blood-soaked nightmare. Yeong-hye’s behavior grows increasingly erratic and self-destructive across the novel’s three equal-length parts, in turn narrated from the perspectives of her husband, brother-in-law, and sister. The narration robs Yeong-hye of her voice and forces readers to watch her plight from a distance, through the viewpoints of those who can’t understand her; a strong sense of alienation pervades the book, in spite of the translator’s poetic prose. The story takes several unsettling and bizarre turns, and the men in the protagonist’s life are monstrous, void of emotion and empathy. The novel feels a bit uneven, with a weak last third and some pacing issues, but Han Kang has crafted an unforgettable plot, if nothing else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10105011908787093495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
