<div id="review_2920972351" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2920972351" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2920972351?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=man-booker">man-booker</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2920972351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer699285111588472524">I read The Vegetarian during a day-long trip on buses, trains and planes, starting at about 8 o'clock in the morning, finishing when the plane touched down an hour late at my final destination. <br/> <br/>Readers, be warned: this experiment should not necessarily be repeated, it may cause utter distress and embarrassment.<br/><br/>8 o'clock, inner city bus in a major German city:<br/><br/>"Before my wife turned vegetarian, I'd always thought of her as completely unremarkable in every way."<br/><br/>I laugh out loud, and gather some su</span>
  <span id="freeText699285111588472524" style="display:none">I read The Vegetarian during a day-long trip on buses, trains and planes, starting at about 8 o'clock in the morning, finishing when the plane touched down an hour late at my final destination. <br /> <br />Readers, be warned: this experiment should not necessarily be repeated, it may cause utter distress and embarrassment.<br /><br />8 o'clock, inner city bus in a major German city:<br /><br />"Before my wife turned vegetarian, I'd always thought of her as completely unremarkable in every way."<br /><br />I laugh out loud, and gather some surprised looks in the "pre-morning-coffee" crowd on the bus. My reaction is somewhat inappropriate, both with regards to my setting and to the fact that this book takes a turn that is NO LAUGHING MATTER! Before buying a coffee-to-go on my first train ride of the day, I already choke on my own laughter, realising that the first of the different narrators is one of the most selfish, uncaring, brutal male prototypes imaginable, and the story is developed from his point of view, which is a magnificent stylistic feature.<br /><br />10 o'clock, crammed local train moving into the conservative countryside:<br /><br />While I read about an atrocious case of physical abuse in the name of patriarchal power, leading to mental breakdown as well as family dissolution, shivering at the passive fictional bystanders watching the violence in voyeuristic fascination, the train prepares for its final station and a group of Asian tourists are lining up in the corridor to leave the train. An elderly Bavarian man tells his wife and grandchild that there is no use getting off their seats as long as (enter derogatory word for Japanese in German which I still am too angry to repeat) are clogging the train. Fuelled by the effect of patriarchal superiority complex I read about just a second earlier, and by the fact that the Asian setting of the novel makes it somehow closer to the situation I am experiencing in real life, I literally see red and turn to the old man, just a casual racist as there are legion everywhere, and ask him if he is aware that all people have the same right to this train? He mumbles something, trying to explain that it is a fact though. They are standing there. Yes, it is a fact that people are standing in the train, trying to leave. Only a racist makes a derogatory remark on their origin (which, by the way, doesn't have to be Japanese at all, just because he thinks so). That is how patriarchy and racism work, and I was shaking when I left the train, as much because of the conflict I had had myself, as because of the enhancing effect of the brutal novel on my fragile equilibrium.<br /><br />12 o'clock in a local village, without cash:<br /><br />While the story evolves into a case of mental disease and a rare form of anorexia nervosa, I start to feel like I am starving myself. Where do you find anything to eat in a village that doesn't accept credit cards, and that thinks vegetarianism means taking the meat off the regular plate? <br /><br />14 o'clock in a local train, other direction, still no food, hallucinating over a chocolate bar:<br /><br />While the train stops for a police action (the story doesn't provide any further information on that matter, unfortunately), and I see minutes pass, worrying I might miss my plane, I look outside and see beautiful landscapes with trees and flowers in abundance, while reading about violent sex acts carried out in a most disturbing way, by protagonists with flower patterns painted all over their bodies. I feel slightly uncomfortable to read the book in a public space now, hoping not to draw any attention to myself. It is intense reading. No quotes.<br /><br />17 o'clock at an airport, waiting, I have food and water and a spot to myself, locked away in the security area:<br /><br />And that is a good thing, because now the story moves between dream and reality, between now and childhood trauma, between guilt and shame. How can you possibly be a woman in a world dominated by male rules and male violence? In a hospital for mental health, the vegetarian tries to change into a plant in order to escape the carnal pain that has defined her life from the beginning. And in her sister, she plants a seed of doubt. What if she is not the strong one, after all? Just the one hiding behind a polished surface, having demured, but not lived?<br /><br />20 o'clock, on a plane, constant background noise as a mother struggles to keep her two toddlers quiet (without success) while the plane has been delayed twice before finally heading to the North:<br /><br />The screams that fill the claustrophobic aircraft cabin resonate in the silent pain of the main character trying to erase the traces of humanity in herself. The story is hardly bearable on a regular day, but after 12 hours of exposure to public transportation, it hits you in the stomach and makes you feel nauseous.<br /><br />22 o'clock, touchdown, last lines.<br /><br />"The look in her eyes is dark and insistent."</span>
  <a data-text-id="699285111588472524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9892765329563936079">Honestly? I don't know what to say. This was one of the hardest books to read, ever.<br/><br/>I started once a few months ago, and at the scene where <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[they FORCED her to eat meat <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I broke.   <span id="freeText9892765329563936079" style="display:none">Honestly? I don't know what to say. This was one of the hardest books to read, ever.<br /><br />I started once a few months ago, and at the scene where <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[they FORCED her to eat meat <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I broke. I could not continue. It was so painful. And I could not imagine the horror of your own family doing that to you. <br /><br />The story is about Yeong-here but except for little snippets (dreams), we follow it through different POV. It had a certain feeling to it like she was degraded from being an adult, a person who can decide for herself. <br /><br />The only thing I really disliked about this book that a)she was not a vegetarian, I'm not even sure that you could call her a vegan, she was very extreme. b)it very much shows her "plant diet" as a mental illness. Yes, she was ill. However, her diet did not cause it, same with anorexia or bulimia, that's usually not the illness, it's how it manifests!<br />Unfortunately, people read this, and some people look at anyone not eating meat and think that they are sick. And some people already told me: "see, she starved because she did not eat meat. And she went crazy! You should get a steak!"<br /><br />FYI, if you have any good book (fiction) with a good representation of people on a plant-based diet, please, write the recommendation in the comments!<br /><br /><br /><i>(Bought in Berlin)</i></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1575382522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10080388631307944718"><i>The Vegetarian</i> is a very cerebral novel. And yet, it's incredibly affecting. Han Kang is able to explore dark and twisted events--and characters--in a really engaging way. It's thrilling to see the story devolve, and I have to say I was never quite sure what was going to happen next. Overall it was such a fun reading experience (which if you've read this book definitely doesn't reflect the subject matter) mostly because the story felt so fresh and exciting. I really can't compare this to anythin</span>
  <span id="freeText10080388631307944718" style="display:none"><i>The Vegetarian</i> is a very cerebral novel. And yet, it's incredibly affecting. Han Kang is able to explore dark and twisted events--and characters--in a really engaging way. It's thrilling to see the story devolve, and I have to say I was never quite sure what was going to happen next. Overall it was such a fun reading experience (which if you've read this book definitely doesn't reflect the subject matter) mostly because the story felt so fresh and exciting. I really can't compare this to anything I've read in a while, and I can see why it recently won the Man Booker International Prize. Try this one if you're looking for something short, powerful and a bit sick. <b>4.5 stars</b></span>

<div id="review_1481440477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1481440477" />
    <a title="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17438949-melissa-dog-wolf-lover-martin"><img alt="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603831478p2/17438949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1481440477?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" href="/user/show/17438949-melissa-dog-wolf-lover-martin">Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17438949-melissa-dog-wolf-lover-martin?shelf=author-pub-giveaways">author-pub-giveaways</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1481440477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9519864405977780084">MY BLOG: <a href='http://melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com/2016/02/the-vegetarian-by-han-kang.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Melissa Martin's Reading List</a><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454034089i/17907377._SX540_.jpg' alt=' : ' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I'm almost at a loss for words on how to review this book. I loved it, it was sad and bizarre, but when dealing with mental illness, not that much is bizarre. I have my own mental illnesses so I don't put too much into what people think because there are those that support you and those that don't. And to me, in my opinion, I think Yeong-hye might not have slipped into this world as far as she did if she had support from her family. One can never tell. <br/><br/>I wis</span>
  <span id="freeText9519864405977780084" style="display:none">MY BLOG: <a href="http://melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com/2016/02/the-vegetarian-by-han-kang.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Melissa Martin's Reading List</a><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454034089i/17907377._SX540_.jpg" alt=" : " class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'm almost at a loss for words on how to review this book. I loved it, it was sad and bizarre, but when dealing with mental illness, not that much is bizarre. I have my own mental illnesses so I don't put too much into what people think because there are those that support you and those that don't. And to me, in my opinion, I think Yeong-hye might not have slipped into this world as far as she did if she had support from her family. One can never tell. <br><br>I wish we could have heard Yeong-hye's thoughts, there were snippets here and there, but this story is told through her husband's eyes, her brother-in-law's eyes and one of her sisters eyes. <br><br>I think her husband was a jerk. He married Yeong-hye because she was plain and wouldn't outshine him. She would be a decent woman to take care of him and she worked her own job without any fuss. But, one night he wakes up and finds her in the kitchen, she's just standing there. He's fussing at her and all she finally says is I had a dream. The whole story line where she is just standing there in the dark like a statue was very creepy to me! <br><br>The next thing her husband finds her throwing out all of the meat and saying she is a vegetarian now. She had a very disturbing dream of blood and death, it doesn't go too much into the dream, but it was enough to change how Yeong-hye saw the world. I really don't know if this was just a beginning of mental illness or something that was just a decision after a dream. <br><br>The sad part is that her husband never supported her, he abused her a few times, her family didn't support her and even abused her. I despised her father for the abuse of an animal and his own daughter. He should have been taking to the gallows, but I digress. I'm not sure, but I'm thinking if they would have supported her and not acting the way they did at a dinner that she might not have ending up in a psych ward for suicidal tendencies. Well, her sorry husband who didn't support her anyway, and was secretly in love with her sister, divorced her. <br><br>I think after Yeong-hye got out of the hospital and lived in her own little flat, that she was getting better. She stopped looking so emaciated because she got to eat all of the veggie food that she wanted. She seemed to be somewhat happy. I was beginning to feel like her luck might be turning around. THEN... her brother-in-law starts coming around, he has a secret lust for her. What is with this family!!! <br><br>I felt like he used her to bring him into his world, to get what he wanted. He was an artist and all of that had to do with body painting. Even still, I'm not sure that Yeong-hye would have went to the extremes she did if her sister didn't walk in on them and have them both carted off to the mental ward because of her jealousy. From this point on, Yeong-hye went downhill... she wouldn't eat, she wanted to be a tree. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1456065574i/18186063.jpg" alt=" : " class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'm really not sure if any of my thoughts on the book are right. I'm not sure if this was something else entirely, but I do know what I took away from the book is that people are cruel. Your own family can be cruel. Sometimes people just need a little help and acceptance, who knows what would have happened. This book is both sad, sensual and yes, a little crazy. But, I loved it. There is just something about it that I loved. <br><br>*I would like to thank The Reading Room and Penguin Random House LLC for a free print copy of this book in exchange for my honest review.*</span>
  <a data-text-id="9519864405977780084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1698723135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9918199406682424797"><i>[Originally appeared here (with edits): <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/life-style/books/features/Review-The-Vegetarian/articleshow/53278560.cms'>http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/li...</a>]</i><br/><br/>Many of us, if stretch a little, can recall the question that appeared in our science textbooks in primary schools: choose the living and non-living thing from the following options. While we conveniently tagged all humans, animals and plants to the ‘living’ side, everything else chugged to the ‘non living’ side. But did the divide stand the test of time?<br/><br/>Han Kang pushes this very divide to scintillating heights, red</span>
  <span id="freeText9918199406682424797" style="display:none"><i>[Originally appeared here (with edits): <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/life-style/books/features/Review-The-Vegetarian/articleshow/53278560.cms">http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/li...</a>]</i><br /><br />Many of us, if stretch a little, can recall the question that appeared in our science textbooks in primary schools: choose the living and non-living thing from the following options. While we conveniently tagged all humans, animals and plants to the ‘living’ side, everything else chugged to the ‘non living’ side. But did the divide stand the test of time?<br /><br />Han Kang pushes this very divide to scintillating heights, reducing the line into a mere fissure, facilitating travel from one living form to another. So, we meet a young Yeong-hye in South Korea, a compliant wife in a patriarchal society, suddenly renouncing meat at the behest of a curious dream. A matter of grave concern, throwing not just her health into jeopardy but also her marital and filial relationships asunder, that must be assuaged before it’s too late. But a series of disturbing, echoing nightmares keep her stoic in her resolve, leaving only her sister and brother-in-law in the big, unforgiving world to extend their support, albeit not without curious pokes and starkly different motives.<br /><br />What follows underline the haunting journey of a woman transformed, repealing everything conventional to assign meaning to her inner voice. Experiencing erotic exploitation and befriending helpless eyes, discarding worldly echoes and embracing floristic world, Yeong-hye moves from a home to a studio to a hospital with incredible equanimity and singular passion <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[to become a <i>plant</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Intermingling three points of view in three different, well-etched parts with the eye of a shrewd player, Kang raises questions on human dichotomies and their constant collision against the inherent shackles of society. The strength of Kang’s voice is in her refusal to smoothen the rough edges of her characters – they bare their scars and innermost vulnerabilities and yet don’t appear drawing sympathy. <a href="http://lithub.com/han-kang-on-violence-beauty-and-the-impossibility-of-innocence/" rel="nofollow noopener">In one of her interviews</a>, Kang ascertains that the novel is an attempt to fight human violence and the possibility to refuse bearing it. A lingering trail formed by each part, almost akin to life that continues to throb long after it has stepped off the page, certainly adds gravitas to her objective.<br /><br />This wasn’t an easy read for me, frequently veering on the bizarre and mystical, puncturing a perfect reading demeanor. But the lithe, supple body of the words came to rescue, lapping up my anxiety like an unexpected drizzle that keeps one’s soul hydrated on a trying road. The sentences, themselves, run like trimmed creepers, embracing the reader in a cocoon of adrenaline rush and propelling slowly towards the destination like an intoxication catching speed.<blockquote>
  <i>"Such uncanny serenity actually frightened him, making him think that perhaps this was a surface impression left behind after any amount of unspeakable viciousness had been digested, or else settled down inside her as a kind of sediment."</i>
</blockquote> The destination, of course, is occasionally a misnomer, for all it does is set us onto a new journey. This book fits to that assertion like a glove.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9918199406682424797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2203971383" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2203971383" />
    <a title="j e w e l s" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68216634-j-e-w-e-l-s"><img alt="j e w e l s" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552606306p2/68216634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2203971383?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="j e w e l s" class="user" itemprop="url" name="j e w e l s" href="/user/show/68216634-j-e-w-e-l-s">j e w e l s</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/29827831-the-vegetarian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68216634-j-e-w-e-l-s?shelf=books-bejeweled">books-bejeweled</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68216634-j-e-w-e-l-s?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2203971383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11022964318969143119"><b>FOUR STARS</b><br/><br/>Call me crazy. I like to challenge myself with "intellectual" novels that win awards. <b>THE VEGETARIAN</b> was definitely demanding and it's another one to add to my <i>appreciate, but didn't exactly enjoy it</i> file. <br/><br/>This South Korean story of an "unremarkable" woman that wakes up from a nightmare and declares herself to be vegetarian is an odd story of rebellion. The woman absolutely infuriates her husband and parents with this assertive decision and somehow becomes a rebel, a subversive, an out</span>
  <span id="freeText11022964318969143119" style="display:none"><b>FOUR STARS</b><br /><br />Call me crazy. I like to challenge myself with "intellectual" novels that win awards. <b>THE VEGETARIAN</b> was definitely demanding and it's another one to add to my <i>appreciate, but didn't exactly enjoy it</i> file. <br /><br />This South Korean story of an "unremarkable" woman that wakes up from a nightmare and declares herself to be vegetarian is an odd story of rebellion. The woman absolutely infuriates her husband and parents with this assertive decision and somehow becomes a rebel, a subversive, an outcast. In the end, she simply wants to be. Without human violence or greediness. Why can't she be like the vegetation she consumes, NOT the carnivorous human society expects of her?<br /><br />Some of the passages about murderous thoughts or bloody animals are not easy to read; but there are also many beautiful passages that are so visual, so visceral--- I felt my heart breaking a little. <br /><br />I listened to the book and loved the production. Poetry should be listened to out loud and this book is perfect for that. Han Kang is a gorgeous writer and I noticed she had another book (THE WHITE BOOK with the same translator) nominated this year for the same award. <br /><br /><b>THE VEGETARIAN</b> owes everything to Kafka and the idea of metamorphosis in life and in death. It's a haunting, complex novel that I'm glad I read. <br /><br /><i>The novel won the 2016 Man Booker International Prize and it is meant to be split between the author (Han Kang) and the translator (Deborah Smith). With good reason. When a novel like this one reads more like poetry, you can understand how translation is of absolute importance. Word choice is EVERYTHING. I googled some info about the translation of this novel and, oh my gosh! I had no idea what I was getting into. According to some Korean American lit critics, Smith took way too many liberties with the translation and the result is a completely different book than the South Koreans read. Apparently, the original is even more poetic and spare and I would imagine, much better. It is an interesting google rabbit hole to fall into if you have a few hours to spare- Haaaaa!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11022964318969143119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1547171884" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1547171884?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="lark benobi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="lark benobi" href="/user/show/73768036-lark-benobi">lark benobi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73768036-lark-benobi?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73768036-lark-benobi?shelf=hogarth">hogarth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73768036-lark-benobi?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1547171884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3647954920068505107">This was a perfect, perfect book for me. The novel is simply told, and very short, but it touches on so many critical, cut-to-the-bone themes, including the most fundamental questions of identity, of gender, of responsibility toward others, and of what makes life worth living. In this novel the more outwardly stable and successful a character is, the less likely they are to have any perspective on their life choices. The more a given character becomes aware of their lived experience, the closer </span>
  <span id="freeText3647954920068505107" style="display:none">This was a perfect, perfect book for me. The novel is simply told, and very short, but it touches on so many critical, cut-to-the-bone themes, including the most fundamental questions of identity, of gender, of responsibility toward others, and of what makes life worth living. In this novel the more outwardly stable and successful a character is, the less likely they are to have any perspective on their life choices. The more a given character becomes aware of their lived experience, the closer he or she comes to falling into the abyss. The story forces characters, and by extension readers, to think deeply about what "self" is, whether it be defined most simply as an organic body with organic desires and needs of its own, or as part of a social structure, where one's value and even one's sanity is defined by others. This was a disturbing read in the best sense possible--I was disturbed from complacent thinking and stirred up with new thoughts. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3647954920068505107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1720553273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720553273" />
    <a title="Nat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23381142-nat"><img alt="Nat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588309995p2/23381142.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720553273?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nat" href="/user/show/23381142-nat">Nat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23381142-nat?shelf=diverse-reads">diverse-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720553273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9309241122629885702">Before the nightmare, Yeong-hye and her husband lived an ordinary life. But when splintering, blood-soaked images start haunting her thoughts, Yeong-hye decides to purge her mind and renounce eating meat.<br/><br/>In a country where societal mores are strictly obeyed, Yeong-hye's decision to embrace a more “plant-like” existence is a shocking act of subversion. And as her passive rebellion manifests in ever more extreme and frightening forms, scandal, abuse, and estrangement begin to send Yeong-hye spiral</span>
  <span id="freeText9309241122629885702" style="display:none">Before the nightmare, Yeong-hye and her husband lived an ordinary life. But when splintering, blood-soaked images start haunting her thoughts, Yeong-hye decides to purge her mind and renounce eating meat.<br><br>In a country where societal mores are strictly obeyed, Yeong-hye's decision to embrace a more “plant-like” existence is a shocking act of subversion. And as her passive rebellion manifests in ever more extreme and frightening forms, scandal, abuse, and estrangement begin to send Yeong-hye spiraling deep into the spaces of her fantasy. In a complete metamorphosis of both mind and body, her now dangerous endeavor will take Yeong-hye—impossibly, ecstatically, tragically—far from her once-known self altogether.<br><br><b>The Vegetarian</b> is told through three parts:<br><br>From here on this review will contain <b>*spoilers*</b>.<br><br><u>Part One</u>: Told through the eyes of the very abusive and unsupportive husband of Yeong-hye (aka Mr. Cheong).<br><br>People who utter bullshit like he did truly astonish me:<br><br><i>“What’s wrong with your lips? Haven’t you done your makeup?”<br>I took my shoes off again and dragged my flustered wife, who’d already put on her coat, into the front room.<br>“Were you really going to go out looking like this?” The two of us were reflected in the dressing table mirror. “Do your makeup again.”</i><br><br>To say that I really, really disliked the husband would be an understatement.<br><br>It was also really disturbing to learn that almost everything seemed to put him into a state of sexual arousal. I mean, just listening to the voice of his sister-in-law managed to arouse him… why???<br><br>On top of that, trigger warnings for sexual assault because Yeong-hye’s husband is a piece of shit.<br><br>Right in those moments, thinking about Mr. Cheong didn’t cause me shock or confusion so much as an intense feeling of disgust. I really hope he'll die a painful death.<br><br>We then see that this abusive asshole calls her family to inform them of Yeong-hye's new eating habits. By this point, they decide that the best way to help is to physically force her into eating meat again, which absolutely made my blood boil.<br><br><i>"That her father, the Vietnam War hero, had actually struck his rebellious daughter in the face and physically forced a lump of meat into her mouth, that was something else."</i><br><br>And the moment when she’d tried to take her own life in front of her family had been a turning point.<br><br><i>“Nobody can help me. Nobody can save me. Nobody can make me breathe.”</i><br><br><u>Part 2</u>: The narrative switches to In-hye's husband (set 2 years later).<br><br>This part was a little disturbing &amp; overly sexual...<br><br>Seriously though, what's up with the men in this book??? We have abusive husband #1 (Mr. Cheong) fantasizing about one sister and abusive husband #2 fantasizing about the other sister... why....<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471672282i/20089237.jpg" width="404" height="230" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The only part of me that was satisfied was when I read that Yeong-hye got her divorce from Mr. Cheong. Thank the stars for that silver lining.<br><br>But I truly felt sorry that In-hye ended up with such a fucker for a husband. And when she had to see him with her sister... it still hurts. But she handled the situation to the best of her abilities.<br><br><i>“She cut him off abruptly, raising her voice. “I’ve called the emergency services.”<br>“What?” He took a step toward her, incomprehension furrowing his brow.<br>She backed away. “You and Yeong-hye are both clearly in need of medical treatment.”<br>Several seconds passed before he grasped that she was in earnest. “What are you saying? That you’re committing me to a mental hospital?”<br>Just then a rustling sound came from over by the mattress. Both he and his wife held their breath. Yeong-hye pushed the sheet aside and stood up, stark naked. He saw that tears were streaming from his wife’s eyes.<br>“Bastard,” she muttered, swallowing her sobs. “Just look at her…she clearly isn’t well. In her mind. How could you?”</i><br><br>Part 2 ends with three paramedics rushing into the flat, concealing straitjackets and protective equipment. Again, a disturbingly dark ending...<br><br><u>Part 3</u>: Told from the perspective of In-hye while visiting her sister in a psychiatric hospital.<br><br>As the story progresses, Yeong-hye became more and more taciturn. She refuses to eat, drink or take care of herself. And it was hard reading about her harming herself like that, especially watching it through In-hye's eyes.<br><br><i>“In-hye couldn’t hold herself back any longer. “You!” she yelled. “I’m acting like this because I’m afraid you’re going to die!”<br>Yeong-hye turned her head and stared blankly at In-hye, as though the latter were not her sister but a complete stranger. After a while, the question came.<br>“Why, is it such a bad thing to die?”</i><br><br>I'm still not entirely sure what ended up with her in those last few pages, but I'm too emotionally scarred to reread it.<br><br>The only silver lining in this part (for me) was reading how strong In-hye held herself to be and not cave in, especially when abusive husband #2 called her in desperation to see the family.<br><br><i>“She’d always known how sensitive he was. A man whose self-esteem was so easily wounded, who quickly became frustrated if the situation didn’t go his way. She knew that if she refused him this one more time, it would probably be a very long time before he contacted her again.<br>Even though she was aware of this, no, because she was aware of it, she hung up without answering.”<br><br>“I don’t know you,” she muttered, tightening her grip on the receiver, which she’d hung back in the cradle but was still clutching. “So there’s no need for us to forgive each other. Because I don’t know you.”<br>When the phone rang again she pulled out the cord. The next morning she connected it up again but, as she’d predicted, he didn’t call again.”</i><br><br>I felt such a surge of power at her actions. <b>YES.</b><br><br>In-hye definitely didn’t give herself enough credit in this part. She’s a single mother with a hard-working business and still takes the time to visit and take care of her younger sister to the best of her abilities... that in itself is already a great deal of work. She shouldn’t be so hard on herself.<br><br><br>Though this novel is short, it managed to feel like a lifetime packed into less than 200 pages - which I'm not sure whether I liked or not.<br><br>But I do know this: I left this novel feeling confused, tired and drained, which I don't find the best things to feel after finishing a story.<br><br>However, the one thing that continues to perplex me is the fact that everyone in this book refers to Yeong-hye as a vegetarian, yet she mentions multiple times that she won’t be eating either eggs or dairy products...<br><br><i>“Just make me some fried eggs. I’m really tired today. I didn’t even get to have a proper lunch.”<br>“I threw the eggs out as well.”<br>“What?”<br>“And I’ve given up milk too.”<br>“This is unbelievable. You’re telling me not to eat meat?”<br>“I couldn’t let those things stay in the fridge. It wouldn’t be right.”</i><br><br>And she even doesn’t wear leather, so why isn’t she considered a vegan?<br><br><i>“The thing is, she’s stopped eating meat.”<br>“What did you say?”<br>“She’s stopped eating any kind of meat at all, even fish—all she lives on is vegetables. It’s been several months now.”</i><br><br>I cannot rest peacefully until I know the reason for this...<br><br><i>*Note: I'm an Amazon Affiliate. If you're interested in buying</i> <b>The Vegetarian</b>, <i>just click on the image below to go through my link. I'll make a small commission!*</i><br><a target="_blank" href="https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0553448188/ref=as_li_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0553448188&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=natalie013-20&amp;linkId=f78b6923beb66882012614f74c1b8535" rel="nofollow noopener">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1822975691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11282400982405885876"><b>Stunning. Sad. Disturbing. Powerful. Unique. Astonishing. Surprising. Profound. Painful. Distressing. Moving. Exceptional. Impacting.</b><br/><br/>This is straight into my Top Ten Reads of 2016! Skipping the long list and the short list. <br/><br/>Wow. Let me catch my breath. No wonder this book won The Man Booker Prize 2016. I've never read anything like it. Finally translated into English for us to treasure and delve into its depths! <br/><br/>Expect nothing when you read this book. Have an open mind. No preconceived concepts.</span>
  <span id="freeText11282400982405885876" style="display:none"><b>Stunning. Sad. Disturbing. Powerful. Unique. Astonishing. Surprising. Profound. Painful. Distressing. Moving. Exceptional. Impacting.</b><br /><br />This is straight into my Top Ten Reads of 2016! Skipping the long list and the short list. <br /><br />Wow. Let me catch my breath. No wonder this book won The Man Booker Prize 2016. I've never read anything like it. Finally translated into English for us to treasure and delve into its depths! <br /><br />Expect nothing when you read this book. Have an open mind. No preconceived concepts. Just let it take you on it's journey. You might not ever be the same again.<br /><br />Tackling mental illness in the most harrowing of ways. <b>Oh God, this book just about broke me in parts.</b>The characters are crystal clear. Our troubled protagonist who becomes vegetarian to the incredibly strong distress of all her family felt like a piece of thin glass ready to break at any moment. Fragile. Flawed. I loved her, I knew her but then I didn't.<br /><br />With erotic and unusual sexual moments the book does make you feel uncomfortable at times. Sometimes I didn't know what to feel. What was appropriate? Some scenes made me want to dive into the book and act out.<br /><br />I'll never forget this book and am eager to read everything and anything this astonishing author writes. <b>The prose is incredible. The plot memorable, tinged with darkness, madness, human suffering. I can't praise this book enough.</b>I'm stunned at low reviews. What book were they reading? This isn't pulp fiction people - this is a literary modern classic. Read it. 5 huge stars. Absolutely astonishing. <br /><br />The ending? Ripped my heart in pieces.</span>

<div id="review_1573033330" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1573033330" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1573033330?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1573033330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10381458900016815504">I liked several aspects of <i>The Vegetarian</i> on an intellectual level. Han Kang does a great job portraying how women suffer from the male gaze, patriarchal standards that make them adhere to men, and abuse brought on by the forced consumption of meat and the men who partake in said consumption. As a future psychologist passionate about eating disorders, though, I abhorred how Kang portrayed Yeong-hye's descent into anorexia. Yes, I get that her refusal to eat meat and then food overall symbolizes </span>
  <span id="freeText10381458900016815504" style="display:none">I liked several aspects of <i>The Vegetarian</i> on an intellectual level. Han Kang does a great job portraying how women suffer from the male gaze, patriarchal standards that make them adhere to men, and abuse brought on by the forced consumption of meat and the men who partake in said consumption. As a future psychologist passionate about eating disorders, though, I abhorred how Kang portrayed Yeong-hye's descent into anorexia. Yes, I get that her refusal to eat meat and then food overall symbolizes her rejection of the normative, sexist values of her surrounding society and family. But Kang glorified this restrictive behavior too much. Others have described this book as "beautiful" and "fresh" and "delicious." <i>This book is literally about a woman who slowly kills herself through self-starvation.</i> There is nothing pretty or romantic or healthy about this novel. I would not want anyone to model their actions after Yeong-hye, and I wish Kang had driven home that point more.<br /><br />Overall, a good depiction of a woman who suffers because of the violence men inflict upon her. I do not recommend it though, based on wanting to avoid the implication that anorexia is a reasonable way to cope - when I know firsthand and from other experiences that it is not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10381458900016815504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1235679767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1235679767" />
    <a title="Caro the Helmet Lady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady"><img alt="Caro the Helmet Lady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571941424p2/7951847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1235679767?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caro the Helmet Lady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caro the Helmet Lady" href="/user/show/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady">Caro the Helmet Lady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846275623" href="/book/show/23206106-the-vegetarian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=madness">madness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=cultural-asia">cultural-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=this-must-be-hell">this-must-be-hell</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=dead-inside">dead-inside</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=disturbia">disturbia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7951847-caro-the-helmet-lady?shelf=foodies">foodies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1235679767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4095455183772424558">I can't say I "liked" this book, a word "appreciated" is more accurate. I appreciated it a lot.<br/><br/>First thought - I am glad I don't live in a country with such level of patriarchal b-shit going on. There were certain excerpts in the story that would drive berserk any <s>western</s> woman. <br/>Nonetheless, this book works on more levels than just that. It is about sexism and discrimination, but it's also about disintegration of family, it's about society and invisible oppression by its norms, it's about mental</span>
  <span id="freeText4095455183772424558" style="display:none">I can't say I "liked" this book, a word "appreciated" is more accurate. I appreciated it a lot.<br /><br />First thought - I am glad I don't live in a country with such level of patriarchal b-shit going on. There were certain excerpts in the story that would drive berserk any <s>western</s> woman. <br />Nonetheless, this book works on more levels than just that. It is about sexism and discrimination, but it's also about disintegration of family, it's about society and invisible oppression by its norms, it's about mental issues and tabooisation of it. It's about tradition that is more important than a person. It's about "what will the <b>neighbours</b> say?".<br /><br />Some people here described this novel as "beautiful", as story of "power of spirit" and such, well, I don't see it this way, I only see one person broken by the system, I see another person killed by the system - in place of "system" you can put whatever you like I used before - family, society, relatives, tradition etc. <br /><br />I liked how Kang in pretty simple words and descriptions - and it's a really short novel - tacked on so many deeply rooted problems. And it's more than just "korean" problems.<br /><br />I mentioned here a tabooisation of mental disorders. So there's another thing about this story that I was thinking of a lot while reading. <br />My very close friend, who used to live in Korean family for many years, occasionally mentions the subject of social stigma against any kind of mental illness in Korean (and Asian) society or how Koreans see seeking for help in such cases as shameful or disgracing and we discuss it often. I found this really interesting interview, called "Stigma of mental illness rooted in Korean culture" by Deepa Bharath (go read it, it's really short <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.ocregister.com/2016/08/29/stigma-of-mental-illness-rooted-in-korean-culture/">http://www.ocregister.com/2016/08/29/...</a>), where the interviewed Mikyong Kim-Goh, professor at Department of Social Work at Calstate Fullerton, says such thing: <br />"<i> ...if you look at Korea, it has a long history of being invaded by other countries. There is a history of oppression and devastation. There is a word in Korean culture to describe this type of “unresolved grief” that affects us as a people – it’s called “han.” This word is very unique to Korean culture. In people who experience han, psychological issues manifest through physical symptoms such as indigestion or insomnia. These people won’t say they are depressed because suffering physical symptoms is more culturally acceptable for them than admitting that something is wrong with their mind.</i>"<br /><br />They call it "han", we call it PTSD. But it definitely puts a finger on where Yeong-hye comes from with her food preferences and her insomnia and I'm sure that's where Kang comes from with her story. On one hand I can say, hey, there was a lot of scary books like this one, she adds absolutely nothing new to the subject of scary stories about tormented souls! But if you look at it through this context, you can say she did a titanic work. She's basically trying to pull the dirty laundry out, wash and dry it in public. You know what I mean? "All eyes on Korea, something stinks in there! I'm Korean and I'm gonna talk about it with the whole world!" So I can understand why this book was controversial and had mixed reviews in Korea.<br /><br />Now, please don't get me wrong, I don't think that other countries have everything clean in their closets. My own country has a problem with suicide prevention, depression among adults and children and other cute things we could write books about (and we do). I'm just saying that this book is worth Man Booker's, even though I not necessarily enjoyed it. But sometimes, in between starships and unicorns, I read books like this one and appreciate it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4095455183772424558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3650561125" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8096076461105778955">i will need to reflect on this book for 3-5 months before i say a single word about it.<br/><br/>review to come / WHO KNOWS THE RATING<br/><br/>-------------<br/><br/>don't mind me, just doing some chill, relaxed reading by picking up a book people have called "terrifying" and "unhinged"</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SOtOsTYu3i15qO6U3fcwcdYoQfWocOcQFkeri/ICmptbeRPwS6RtueOxqelkbdyu18ygd3MD9FpnJw5p+f6jfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ic54/4ghh71xETPmBsTrfgvI5l3sHIvdwby2e6Y2rVcyXCW+9as0KesIdJu/XgehCiwH3zdvmJew3BOZrcqUsQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

묘사한다. 이를 통해 작가는 제도적 성차별이 줄어든 시대의 보이지 않는 차별들이 어떻게 여성들의 삶을 제약하고 억압하는지 보여 준다. 여권이 신장된 시대, 그러나 여전히 ‘여성’이라는 조건이 굴레로 존재하는 사회에서 살아가는 한 여자의 인생을 다룬 『82년생 김지영』은 조용한 고백과 뜨거운 고발로 완성된 새로운 페미니즘 소설이자 수많은 사람들의 경험과 <span id="freeTextContainer9602479865719775145">소설가 <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18166474.___' title=' 한강' rel='nofollow noopener'> 한강</a><br/><br/>Han Kang is the daughter of novelist Han Seung-won. She was born in Kwangju and at the age of 10, moved to Suyuri (which she speaks of affectionately in her work "Greek Lessons") in Seoul. <br/><br/>She studied Korean literature at Yonsei University. She began her writing career when one of her poems was featured in the winter issue of the quarterly Literature and Society. She made her official liter</span>
  <span id="freeText9602479865719775145" style="display:none">소설가 <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18166474.___" title=" 한강" rel="nofollow noopener"> 한강</a><br /><br />Han Kang is the daughter of novelist Han Seung-won. She was born in Kwangju and at the age of 10, moved to Suyuri (which she speaks of affectionately in her work "Greek Lessons") in Seoul. <br /><br />She studied Korean literature at Yonsei University. She began her writing career when one of her poems was featured in the winter issue of the quarterly Literature and Society. She made her official literary debut in the following year when her short story "The Scarlet Anchor" was the winning entry in the daily Seoul Shinmun spring literary contest. <br /><br />Since then, she has gone on to win the Yi Sang Literary Prize (2005), Today's Young Artist Award, and the Korean Literature Novel Award. As of summer 2013, Han teaches creative writing at the Seoul Institute of the Arts while writing stories and novels.</span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/18449744">Quotes from The Vegetarian</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The feeling that she had never really lived in this world caught her by surprise. It was a fact. She had never lived. Even as a child, as far back as she could remember, she had done nothing but endure. She had believed in her own inherent goodness, her humanity, and lived accordingly, never causing anyone harm. Her devotion to doing things the right way had been unflagging, all her successes had depended on it, and she would have gone on like that indefinitely. She didn't understand why, but faced with those decaying buildings and straggling grasses, she was nothing but a child who had never lived.&rdquo;</span>
